# The Consumer as "Serious Child": A Theoretical Analysis of Systemic Infantilization and Its Discontents

## Abstract

This article develops a theoretical framework for understanding the
Western consumer not as a sovereign adult actor but as a "serious
child"---a subject systematically conditioned to occupy a permanent
state of dependent adolescence within what Benjamin Barber terms the
"infantilist ethos" of consumer capitalism. Drawing upon critical theory
from Herbert Marcuse's analysis of repressive desublimation to Jean
Baudrillard's concept of hyperreality, and integrating contemporary
scholarship on "life stage dissolution," this paper argues that the
consumer subject experiences a transfer of authority from familial to
market structures rather than genuine emancipation. The analysis further
examines the fate of those who achieve critical consciousness---the
"Emergent Adult"---and demonstrates how social outcasts, the homeless,
and the drug-addicted represent not failures of the system but its
necessary byproducts and living indictments. The paper concludes by
reframing the condition not as immaturity but as systemic violation,
proposing that the language of sovereignty and consent must be radically
rethought in an age of manufactured subjectivity.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 1. Introduction: Beyond the Sovereign Consumer

The foundational myth of neoliberal economics and liberal political
theory posits the consumer as a rational, autonomous agent---an adult
capable of making informed choices in the marketplace of goods and
ideas. This figure, descending from Adam Smith's rational actor and John
Stuart Mill's self-determining individual, remains the normative
baseline against which deviation is measured as pathology or failure .
Yet a growing body of critical scholarship suggests that this model
bears increasingly little relation to the actual subject produced by
advanced consumer capitalism.

This article advances a contrary thesis: the Western consumer, while
possessing the legal attributes of adulthood, functions psychologically
and socially as what might be termed a "serious child"---an individual
whose apparent maturity masks a fundamental dependency on external
validation, whose agency operates within parameters so narrowly
circumscribed as to render the concept of freedom problematic, and whose
very desires are manufactured by the systems that purport to serve them.
This is not a failure of individual development but a systemic
achievement---the deliberate production of a subject suited to the
requirements of a consumption-driven economy.

The argument proceeds in four movements. First, it establishes the
theoretical foundations for understanding consumer society as a system
of managed immaturity, drawing upon Marcuse, Baudrillard, and
contemporary theorists of infantilization. Second, it examines the
mechanisms through which this system operates---the "enclosure" that
replaces familial authority with market discipline. Third, it analyzes
the fate of those who achieve critical consciousness, demonstrating how
social marginalization functions as an immune response to authentic
adulthood. Finally, it confronts the ethical implications of framing
this condition as something more profound than immaturity: as a
violation of the very possibility of selfhood.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 2. Theoretical Foundations: The Manufactured Subject

### 2.1 Marcuse and the Collapse of Transcendence

Herbert Marcuse's *One-Dimensional Man* (1964) provides the essential
starting point for understanding how advanced industrial society
produces subjects incapable of imagining alternatives to the existing
order. Marcuse's signal contribution lies in his diagnosis of how
material abundance functions as an instrument of political and
psychological containment. As Lucien Goldmann's contemporary analysis
noted, Marcuse demonstrated that "while the extraordinary productivity
of contemporary society has reduced necessary repression to a minimum,
the structure of corporate capitalism and consumer society has greatly
increased effective repression" .

The concept of "repressive desublimation" captures the dialectical trap
at the heart of consumer culture. Marcuse writes that "\[t\]his society
turns everything it touches into a potential source of progress and of
exploitation, of drudgery and satisfaction, of freedom and oppression" .
What appears as liberation---the availability of once-forbidden
pleasures, the saturation of everyday life with erotic imagery, the
promise of instant gratification---actually serves to foreclose deeper
forms of freedom. The subject is permitted every satisfaction except the
satisfaction of genuine autonomy. As contemporary scholarship on Marcuse
explains, "Consumerism arouses a dialectical conversion whereby the
eroticism of 'sex sells' produces the thanatoid insight that packaging
always trumps product. The tumescence consumers experience at the cash
register... quickly results in the detumescent eroticism of nervous
laughter" .

This analysis fundamentally challenges the notion that consumer choice
represents freedom. Rather, the subject confronts a field of options
that are predetermined in their basic structure---a menu rather than an
open horizon. The laughter that follows the purchase is the recognition,
however fleeting, that the promised satisfaction will not arrive. Yet
this recognition produces not transcendence but a kind of knowing
resignation, a theme to which we will return.

### 2.2 Baudrillard and the Vanishing of the Real

If Marcuse diagnoses the psychological mechanism of containment, Jean
Baudrillard traces its ontological consequences. The trajectory of
Baudrillard's work maps the progressive disappearance of any stable
reality against which the simulations of consumer culture might be
measured. In his four-stage genealogy of the image, representation moves
from reflecting a basic reality to masking and perverting it, to masking
the absence of reality, and finally to bearing "no relation to any
reality whatever" .

The contemporary moment, Baudrillard argues, is defined by the
achievement of "hyperreality"---a condition in which the copy precedes
and determines the original, in which Disneyland functions not as an
escape from America but as a advertisement for America's claim to be
real. This is not, as popular appropriations of Baudrillard would have
it, a science-fiction scenario of virtual worlds replacing the physical.
It is rather the more profound insight that "the real has only ever been
a form of simulation" . What we take for reality is itself a construct,
and the proliferation of digital copies, immersive metaverses, and
AI-generated content merely makes visible what was always the case.

For the consumer subject, this has devastating implications. The
"symbolic dimension of life"---the realm of meaning, ritual, and genuine
social bond---is progressively absorbed into what Baudrillard calls
"integral reality," a total system of operationalized, digitized,
commodified experience . The subject does not confront a world of things
with which to engage authentically but a world of signs that refer only
to other signs. Consumption becomes not the satisfaction of needs but
the manipulation of signifiers, a game whose rules are set by the system
itself.

### 2.3 Infantilization as Systemic Logic

The concept of infantilization bridges the psychological insights of
Marcuse with the ontological analysis of Baudrillard. Benjamin Barber's
*Consumed: How Markets Corrupt Children, Infantilize Adults, and Swallow
Citizens Whole* (2007) provides the most systematic treatment of this
theme. Barber traces the emergence of what he terms the "infantilist
ethos"---a cultural logic that "markets corrupt children, infantilize
adults, and swallow citizens whole" .

The trajectory Barber describes moves from the Protestant ethic of
delayed gratification and productive labor to a "puerility" that
celebrates impulsivity, narcissism, and perpetual dissatisfaction. This
is not an accidental cultural shift but a functional requirement of an
economic system that must generate ever-increasing consumption. The
mature adult who finds satisfaction in work, community, and family is a
poor consumer. The "kidult"---the adult who retains the tastes, desires,
and psychological structure of the child---is an ideal market
participant.

Contemporary scholarship on "life stage dissolution" confirms this
diagnosis. Rather than the emergence of a genuine new stage of "emerging
adulthood," what we observe is "the erosion of established ones"---a
process involving the bidirectional dynamics of "adultification"
(children pressed into premature consumer roles) and "infantilization"
(adults retaining childlike patterns of desire and dependence) . This
blurring of traditionally demarcated life stages serves the interests of
"marketing practices that actively seek to erode traditionally
demarcated adult and childhood roles, differences, and oppositions as a
new and distinct message within contemporary consumerism" .

The "serious child" thus emerges as the characteristic subject of
advanced consumer capitalism---an individual who possesses the economic
responsibilities of adulthood (work, debt, insurance) while occupying
the psychological position of the child (dependency on external
validation, incapacity for genuine autonomy, orientation toward
immediate gratification). This is not a failure of development but its
successful completion within the terms set by the system.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 3. Mechanisms of Capture: From Family to Market

### 3.1 The Transfer of Authority

The transition from childhood to "adulthood" in consumer society is
properly understood not as emancipation but as the transfer of authority
from one enclosing structure to another. The family, whatever its
pathologies, at least offers the possibility of genuine relationship, of
care that is not entirely reducible to exchange. The market offers only
transactions.

This transfer operates through what John Kenneth Galbraith termed the
"dependence effect"---the insight that "consumers' desires for goods and
services are not intrinsic but that the production side creates consumer
desire" through advertising that "adorns new commodities with more
expressive and evocative meanings" . The wants that drive consumption
depend upon the process by which they are satisfied. The subject does
not bring pre-existing needs to the market; the market manufactures
needs and then offers their satisfaction.

David Riesman's analysis of social character types in *The Lonely Crowd*
(1950) traces the psychological correlate of this economic
transformation. Riesman identified a shift from "inner-directed"
character (guided by internalized values) to "other-directed" character
(oriented toward the expectations and preferences of peer groups). In
the context of consumer society, this other-direction produces what
Riesman called the "standard package"---"the process of compulsive
acquisition of commodities" that creates "a considerable degree of
uniformity throughout American society" .

The "serious child" thus seeks not what they authentically desire---a
concept that becomes increasingly problematic within this
framework---but what will secure the approval of others, what conforms
to the expectations of the reference group, what the advertising image
promises will complete them. This is precisely the psychological
structure of the child seeking parental approval, transferred to the
larger playground of the market.

### 3.2 The Saturated Society

Pekka Sulkunen's concept of the "saturated society" illuminates the
political dimension of this transformation. In a saturated society, "the
modern ideals of progress, individualism, and the democratic state" have
reached their limits, and lifestyle regulation is reduced to "stressing
the self-responsibility of individuals within a free market" . The
subject is formally free---free to choose among options, free to succeed
or fail---but this freedom operates within a framework that is itself
beyond question.

Sulkunen's analysis draws upon the sociological tradition from Adam
Smith through Ulrich Beck and Anthony Giddens to trace how "the power of
autonomy" becomes a mechanism of control rather than liberation . The
individual who believes themselves to be exercising free choice is
actually performing the choices that the system requires. This is what
Marcuse meant by "one-dimensional man"---"well paid, comfortable,
knowing only how to execute other people's decisions, and consume" .

### 3.3 Manufactured Dissatisfaction

The system's genius lies in its capacity to generate dissatisfaction as
a renewable resource. As contemporary philosophical analysis notes, "the
current state of consumer capitalism causes those in it to be stuck in a
cycle that creates and utilizes boredom to promote dissatisfaction in
life and increase consumption" . Boredom becomes "a phenomenon in
people's lives that is used as a method of manipulation within the
consumer capitalist system... taught to be something undesirable to run
away from, which leads people to flee to consumption" .

This cycle is not accidental but structural. The system requires that
satisfaction remain perpetually deferred---the next purchase promising
what the last one failed to deliver. The "serious child" lives in a
state of permanent disappointment that paradoxically fuels continued
participation. To achieve genuine satisfaction would be to exit the
cycle; the system therefore ensures that satisfaction never arrives.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 4. The Fate of the Emergent Adult: Social Outcasts as Systemic Necessity

### 4.1 The Waking Wound

What becomes of the subject who achieves critical consciousness---who
recognizes the mechanisms of capture and attempts to live outside them?
The "Emergent Adult" (as we might term this figure) confronts a world
that has no place for them and a set of immune responses designed to
neutralize the threat they pose.

The path to emergence is necessarily traumatic. To awaken from the
consumer dreamscape is to feel, for the first time, the pain that
consumption was designed to numb: the existential terror of
meaninglessness, the isolation of atomized social existence, the weight
of geopolitical and ecological realities that consumer culture exists to
obscure. The subject who achieves this recognition does so not through
intellectual exercise alone but through an experience of wounding---the
collapse of the defenses that made everyday life bearable.

This is why André Gorz's analysis of "consumption alienation" remains
essential. Gorz argues that consumer society is a system in which
"commodities manufacture consumers" rather than the reverse, and that
"capitalist economic growth actually produces scarcity" rather than
abundance . The alienation of consumption is continuous with the
alienation of labor; both serve the reproduction of a system that
requires subjects incapable of genuine satisfaction. To recognize this
is to find oneself radically alone, stripped of the consolations that
the culture provides.

### 4.2 The Three Immune Responses

The society of the "serious child" possesses three distinct mechanisms
for neutralizing the Emergent Adult.

**Co-optation** is the first and most sophisticated response. The market
absorbs all critique, turning rebellion into a lifestyle option,
authenticity into a brand. The Emergent Adult's rejection of consumerism
becomes "minimalism," a aesthetic choice available for purchase. Their
search for spiritual meaning becomes "mindfulness," an app subscription.
Their handmade clothes become "artisanal," a premium product. As
Baudrillard's analysis of simulation would predict, the system
incorporates its own negation, rendering critique indistinguishable from
participation.

**Othering** is the second response, deployed when co-optation fails.
The Emergent Adult who cannot be absorbed must be made wrong---labeled
as a failure, a loser, someone who "couldn't handle the real world."
This mechanism serves to reinforce the boundaries of normalcy, providing
the "serious children" with reassurance that their own path is the only
viable one. The outcast functions as a cautionary tale, a living warning
against the dangers of thinking too much or wanting too much freedom.

**Pathologization** is the third response, reserved for those who are
neither co-opted nor simply rejected but who embody the wound of
emergence in visible form. The homeless, the drug-addicted, the severely
mentally ill---these figures are not, from this perspective, simply
unfortunates who fell through the cracks. They are the necessary
casualties of a system that requires most of its members to remain
asleep. Their existence is not a failure of the system but its
successful operation, producing the wreckage that confirms the wisdom of
staying within the bounds.

### 4.3 The Homeless and the Addict as Living Indictment

This framework requires us to radically reframe our understanding of
homelessness and addiction. The homeless person is not merely someone
without housing but someone who has been expelled from the consumer role
altogether---the ultimate "non-consumer" whose existence is a standing
rebuke to the system's claim to provide for all. The response to
homelessness---criminalization, removal, the construction of hostile
architecture---reveals the system's true relation to those who cannot or
will not participate.

The drug addict presents a more complex case. The addict has found an
alternative anesthetic, one that operates outside the market's approved
channels. Where the "serious child" uses shopping, streaming services,
and social media to manage the pain of existence, the addict uses
substances that provide more immediate---and more dangerous---relief.
The addict is not the opposite of the consumer but their shadow, the one
who has taken the logic of chemical management of consciousness to its
logical extreme. The fury directed at addicts reflects the threat they
pose: they reveal that the line between "responsible" consumption and
"addictive" consumption is arbitrary, a matter of social convention
rather than essential difference.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 5. Beyond Metaphor: Systemic Violation and the Question of Consent

### 5.1 The Limits of the Infantilization Frame

To this point, this analysis has operated within the terms of the
infantilization metaphor---the "serious child" as the characteristic
subject of consumer capitalism. But this frame, however analytically
useful, risks obscuring the gravity of what is being described. To call
someone a child is, in certain contexts, to diminish the violence done
to them. Children are, after all, supposed to be dependent. Their lack
of autonomy is developmentally appropriate.

The subject of consumer capitalism is not a child. They possess the full
cognitive and emotional equipment of the adult, systematically turned
against itself. Their dependency is not natural but manufactured. Their
desires are not their own. Their choices are not free. This condition
requires a language adequate to its violence.

### 5.2 Rape as Analogy: The Violation of Sovereignty

The analogy of rape, however jarring, captures dimensions of the
experience that the infantilization metaphor cannot. Rape is not
primarily about sex but about the violation of sovereignty---the
imposition of another's will on the most intimate territory of the self.
The consumer subject has been similarly violated, not in the body but in
the psyche. The system does not ask what they want to be, what they
value, how they wish to connect. It imposes an identity: consumer,
worker, taxpayer, unit of economic activity.

This violation is ongoing and structural. It does not occur as a
discrete event but as a continuous process of subject formation---the
education of desire, the shaping of attention, the colonization of the
imagination. The subject is not consulted about any of this. They are
born into a world that is already arranged, already saturated with
meaning, already demanding their participation.

The demand for gratitude compounds the violation. The system presents
its chains as gifts---comfort, convenience, entertainment, choice. It
insists that the subject should be grateful for what it provides, should
recognize how much worse things could be, should count their blessings
and stop complaining. This is precisely the dynamic of the abusive
relationship: the abuser frames control as care, demands gratitude for
the violence they inflict, and pathologizes the victim's attempts to
escape.

### 5.3 Toward a Politics of Emergence

If this analysis is even partially correct, it suggests that the task of
critical theory and political practice is not reform but emergence---the
creation of conditions under which genuine adulthood becomes possible.
This is necessarily a collective project. No one achieves critical
consciousness alone; no one sustains it without community. The Emergent
Adult requires others who have made the same journey, who can provide
the recognition and support that the system denies.

This is also necessarily a negative project, at least initially. One
cannot build the new within the shell of the old. One can only
refuse---refuse the identities on offer, refuse the choices presented,
refuse the satisfactions promised. This refusal is not itself freedom
but its precondition. It clears space for something else to emerge.

What that something else might be remains to be discovered. The
anthropological record offers hints---societies organized around gift
rather than commodity, around relationship rather than transaction,
around sufficiency rather than accumulation. The "original affluent
society" of hunter-gatherers, who achieved remarkable satisfaction with
minimal labor, stands as a permanent challenge to the consumerist
assumption that more is always better . But we cannot simply return to
the past. We must find our own way forward.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

## 6. Conclusion: The Ghosts at the Feast

This analysis has argued that the Western consumer is best understood
not as a sovereign adult but as a "serious child"---a subject
systematically conditioned to occupy a permanent state of dependent
adolescence. This condition is not an accident or a failure of
individual development but a systemic achievement, the deliberate
production of a subject suited to the requirements of a
consumption-driven economy.

The theoretical tradition from Marcuse through Baudrillard to
contemporary scholars of infantilization provides the resources for
understanding this condition. Marcuse shows how material abundance
functions as psychological containment. Baudrillard traces the
disappearance of any reality against which the simulations of consumer
culture might be measured. Barber and others document the deliberate
cultivation of infantilism as an economic strategy.

The fate of those who achieve critical consciousness---the Emergent
Adults---reveals the system's immune responses: co-optation for those
who can be absorbed, othering for those who cannot, pathologization for
the casualties of emergence. The homeless and the addict are not
failures of the system but its necessary byproducts, the ghosts at the
feast whose presence confirms the wisdom of staying within the bounds.

The language of violation---rape as analogy---captures dimensions of
this condition that the infantilization metaphor cannot. The subject of
consumer capitalism has been violated in their capacity for
self-determination, their sovereignty over their own psyche, their
access to genuine satisfaction. The demand that they be grateful for
their chains compounds the violation.

What remains is the question of what might come next. If the system is
as total as this analysis suggests, if it captures every dimension of
human existence and turns every negation into affirmation, then the
possibility of genuine escape seems foreclosed. And yet the very
existence of this analysis, the very fact that such questions can be
asked, suggests that the system is not quite total. There are cracks.
There are refusals. There are Emergent Adults, however few, however
precarious.

The task, perhaps, is to find each other---to build the communities of
recognition and support that make emergence sustainable, to create the
spaces where genuine adulthood becomes possible, to keep faith with the
possibility of a life not entirely captured by the demands of the
market. This is not a political program in any conventional sense. It is
something more modest and more fundamental: the work of becoming human
in an inhuman age.

------------------------------------------------------------------------

256.

Gorz, A. (2009). Consumption alienation, ecology crisis, and system
critics. *Marxist Studies*, (4).

Marcuse, H. (1964). *One-dimensional man: Studies in the ideology of
advanced industrial society*. Beacon Press.

Riesman, D. (1950). *The lonely crowd: A study of the changing American
character*. Yale University Press.

Smith, R. G. (2025). Applied Baudrillard: From the virtual economy of
